disk-backed virtual memory.  Nothing has moved to CUDA or Apple Metal
Performance Shaders (MPS).  Device
placement occurs later, after the geometry exists and the batching code knows
which rows a particular minibatch needs.

\subsubsection{The first row-identity check}

The loader immediately compares the row counts:
\begin{equation}
  C.\mathrm{shape}[0] = V.\mathrm{shape}[0].
\end{equation}
If the counts differ, it raises an error that names both files and both
counts.  Equality is necessary but not sufficient.  It proves that the files
have the same number of rows; it cannot prove by itself that row $i$ in one
file belongs to row $i$ in the other.  The generator must preserve that
ordering when it writes the pair.

\subsection{A seeded permutation, followed by physical cuts}

The loader next creates a permutation of the integers $0,\ldots,N-1$:
\begin{verbatim}
order_tensor = torch.randperm(
    n,
    generator=gen)
order = order_tensor.numpy()
\end{verbatim}
\code{torch.randperm} returns a one-dimensional PyTorch tensor containing
every integer exactly once in pseudorandom order.  It is not an iterator and
it is not lazy: all $N$ indices are materialized.  The call to
\code{.numpy()} exposes the CPU tensor as a NumPy array.  Because both objects
use CPU storage, this conversion normally shares memory rather than copying
the index values.

The physical-window function receives $C$, the permutation, and the ordered
parameter names.  Each active window locates its columns by name and computes
one derived quantity per candidate row:
\begin{align}
  h &\equiv
  \frac{H_0}{100\,{\rm km}\,{\rm s}^{-1}\,{\rm Mpc}^{-1}},\\
  \omega_b &\equiv \Omega_b h^2,\\
  q_{\tt omegam2h2} &\equiv (\Omega_m h)^2,\\
  \omega_m &\equiv \Omega_m h^2,\\
  q_{\tt omegamns} &\equiv \omega_m n_s.
\end{align}
The symbol $\omega_m$ is the usual physical matter density $\Omega_m h^2$.
The symbol $n_s$ is the dimensionless scalar spectral index: it describes the
tilt of the primordial curvature-power spectrum relative to a scale-invariant
spectrum.  It is a cosmological parameter column, not the number of samples.
The separately named configuration quantity \code{omegam2h2} is
$(\Omega_m h)^2=\Omega_m^2h^2$ and is therefore not another spelling of
$\omega_m$.  Naming both prevents a factor of $\Omega_m$ from disappearing in
a cut review.
For an active lower bound $a$ and upper bound $b$, the comparison is strict:
$a<q_i<b$.  The code builds a Boolean mask for each window and intersects it
with the masks already applied.  A Boolean mask is an array of \code{True}
and \code{False} values, one per candidate row.  Indexing
\code{order[mask]} uses NumPy advanced indexing and creates a new array of
the surviving integer indices.  Their order remains the shuffled order.

The result, called \code{phys}, is therefore not a new cosmology table.  It is
a list of row coordinates into the original files:
\begin{equation*}
\begin{aligned}
  \code{phys} &= [i_0,i_1,\ldots],\\
  C[i_k]&\text{ and }V[i_k]\text{ remain a matched pair.}
\end{aligned}
\end{equation*}

\subsection{Selecting an absolute number of rows}

The YAML states an absolute number of training rows, \code{n\_train}, and an
absolute number of validation rows, \code{n\_val}.  After the cuts, the
loader checks that the requested number is at least one and that the physical
pool is large enough.  It then takes
\begin{verbatim}
idx = phys[:n_keep]
\end{verbatim}
The slice is the first $n_{\rm keep}$ entries of the seeded, cut permutation.
Using the same seed at multiple training sizes creates nested learning-curve
subsets: the $2{,}000$-row set is the prefix of the $4{,}000$-row set rather
than an unrelated draw.

Training and validation use different files.  Reusing the numerical seed
therefore repeats the selection algorithm, not the cosmologies themselves.
The scientific assumption is that the two file sets are disjoint.  That
assumption must be checked from file identity or row identity; a seed does not
establish disjointness.

\subsection{Staging: copy the subset or retain the disk mapping}

The word \emph{stage} means ``put the selected rows in the storage form from
which later batches will read them.''  It does not yet mean ``move them to the
GPU.''  The helper \code{stage\_source} estimates whether the selected rows
fit within a configured fraction of available host memory.  The estimate
adds three allocations: the compact parameter array, the compact data-vector
array, and the integer reindex array.  Each numerical term uses the dtype and
column count of the stored array it describes.  The branch decision uses all
three terms.  The current status line prints the parameter, data-vector, and
index contributions separately, followed by their total, the available-memory
budget, the exact comparison, and the chosen resident or disk branch.  The
worked calculation below derives those same three contributions so the banner
can be checked rather than merely trusted.

There are two outcomes.

\subsubsection{Resident host-memory outcome}

If the pair fits, the helper gathers the sorted unique selected rows into
compact arrays.  Advanced integer indexing is eager and returns a copy.  The
compact arrays have shapes
\begin{equation}
  C_{\rm src}\in\mathbb{R}^{n_u\times P},
  \qquad
  V_{\rm src}\in\mathbb{R}^{n_u\times D},
\end{equation}
where $n_u$ is the number of distinct selected rows.  The source's
\code{idx} is then expressed in the compact arrays' local row coordinates.
If original dump row 91 becomes compact row 3, later code asks the resident
array for row 3, not row 91.

\subsubsection{Disk-backed outcome}

If the data vectors do not fit, the helper retains the original read-only
\code{np.memmap}.  The source's \code{idx} remains in global on-disk row
coordinates.  A later batch read such as \code{dv[idx]} asks the operating
system for only those rows.  This advanced indexing operation materializes
the requested batch; it does not turn the entire dump into an in-memory
array.

The parameter table was already loaded eagerly by \code{np.loadtxt}.  Thus,
``disk-backed source'' describes the data vectors, not every object in the
source dictionary.

\subsection{The source dictionary and its coordinate systems}

\code{load\_source} returns a small dictionary:
\begin{verbatim}
dump_rows = sorted_unique_original_rows
src = {"C":         C_src,
       "dv":        dv_src,
       "idx":       idx_src,
       "dump_rows": dump_rows}
\end{verbatim}

The fields have distinct jobs.

\begin{table*}[t]
\centering
\begin{tabular}{p{0.18\textwidth}p{0.25\textwidth}p{0.47\textwidth}}
\toprule
Key & Coordinate system & Meaning \\
\midrule
\code{C}
  & Matches \code{idx}
  & Parameter rows.  It is compact in the resident outcome and the full
    eager table in the disk-backed outcome. \\
\code{dv}
  & Matches \code{idx}
  & Data-vector rows.  It is compact in the resident outcome and a file
    mapping in the disk-backed outcome. \\
\code{idx}
  & Local or global
  & The row coordinates that consumers must use with this particular
    \code{C}/\code{dv} pair. \\
\code{dump\_rows}
  & Always global
  & Sorted unique row numbers in the original files.  A sibling dump uses
    these coordinates to retrieve the same cosmologies. \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\caption{Keys in a staged source dictionary.  ``Local'' coordinates address a
compact resident copy; ``global'' coordinates address original disk rows.
Consumers index \code{C} and \code{dv} only with the accompanying \code{idx};
\code{dump\_rows} exists for a separate sibling file that still uses the
original coordinate system.}
\label{tab:source-dictionary}
\end{table*}

\code{dump\_rows} is needed by the two-dimensional matter-power path.  That
path has a second, row-aligned base dump.  Even when staging has converted
the primary arrays to local coordinates, the sibling file still needs the
original disk row numbers.  Sorting makes its reads sequential.  Applying
\code{np.unique} removes duplicates and also sorts; it returns a new array.

Figure~\ref{fig:row-coordinates} shows the branch with a concrete selection.
The selected rows are unique because they are a prefix of a permutation.  The
order $[9,2,5]$ is deliberately not sorted: it lets the figure distinguish
training-selection order from efficient disk-read order.

\begin{figure*}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.94\textwidth]
  {texnotes/figures/fig04_row_coordinates.pdf}
\caption{One selection, two storage representations, and two coordinate
systems.  A global row addresses the original files; a local row addresses a
compact copy.  \code{idx\_src} must be interpreted with the returned
\code{C}/\code{dv} pair.  \code{dump\_rows} never becomes local because the
grid2d base file is addressed in original-file coordinates.}
\label{fig:row-coordinates}
\end{figure*}

\subsection{Training statistics are owned by the training source}

For the training source, \code{with\_means=True}.  The loader computes the
mean of every parameter column and every staged data-vector column:
\begin{equation}
  \bar C_j = \frac{1}{N_{\rm tr}}\sum_{i=1}^{N_{\rm tr}}C_{ij},
  \qquad
  \bar V_k = \frac{1}{N_{\rm tr}}\sum_{i=1}^{N_{\rm tr}}V_{ik}.
\end{equation}
Here $N_{\rm tr}$ is the number of selected training cosmologies.  The
parameter index $j$ runs over raw parameter columns, whereas the target
index $k$ runs over data-vector columns.  Validation rows are not included
in either sum: using them would let the held-out set influence the coordinate
system used to judge it.
These arrays are stored as \code{C\_mean} and \code{dv\_mean}.  The
validation source uses \code{with\_means=False}; it does not estimate a
second zero point.  Training and validation must be expressed in the same
coordinate system, so the training-derived geometry transforms both.

Subtracting a center does not change a residual-based loss.  If prediction
and truth are both centered by $\bar V$, then
\begin{equation}
  (V_{\rm pred}-\bar V)-(V_{\rm true}-\bar V)
  = V_{\rm pred}-V_{\rm true}.
\end{equation}
Centering changes the zero point the network learns, not the physical
prediction-minus-truth residual.

The matter-power path is again special.  Its raw surface is transformed into
``law space''---for example, $\log(P/P_{\rm base})$---and may be thinned along
the wavenumber axis.  Its final output mean must therefore be computed after
that transformation.  The generic loader skips the raw \code{dv\_mean}; the
bounded matter-power staging code streams the transformed mean and variance
over row chunks.

\subsection{Worked staging example: the same rows in two coordinate systems}

Return to the four-row example in Sec.~\ref{sec:reader-map}.  Suppose the
seeded permutation is
\begin{equation*}
  \code{order}=[2,0,3,1]
\end{equation*}
and a physical cut rejects original row 0.  The surviving pool is
\begin{equation*}
  \code{phys}=[2,3,1].
\end{equation*}
If \code{n\_keep=2}, the selected global rows are $[2,3]$.  Before staging,
the following expressions all refer to the original files:
\begin{align*}
  C[2]&=(0.33,73,0.052),\\
  C[3]&=(0.30,69,0.049),\\
  V[2]&=(12,24,36,48,60),\\
  V[3]&=(10.5,21,31.5,42,52.5).
\end{align*}

If the subset fits in host RAM, \code{stage\_source} makes compact copies over
the distinct rows in sorted order:
\begin{equation*}
 C_{\rm src}=C[[2,3]],\qquad V_{\rm src}=V[[2,3]].
\end{equation*}
The new arrays have two rows.  The returned \code{idx} is the local coordinate
of each selected row, in the original selected order, inside that compact
copy: $\code{searchsorted}([2,3],[2,3])=[0,1]$.  This selection is already
sorted, so the map happens to be the identity; the next paragraph shows the
general case.  \code{dump\_rows} remains $[2,3]$ because a separately opened
base dump still uses original file coordinates.

If the subset does not fit, the program returns the original $C$ and memmapped
$V$ with \code{idx=[2,3]}.  No compact copy was made.  A consumer must always
apply the \code{idx} returned with its particular arrays.  Reusing the
resident branch's local coordinates against the file-backed branch would
silently select the wrong cosmologies.

An ordinary selection is unsorted, and the two branches must still present its
rows in one canonical order.  For $[9,2,5]$, the resident copy is stored
sorted as $[2,5,9]$, but the returned local index is
$\code{searchsorted}([2,5,9],[9,2,5])=[2,0,1]$, \emph{not} a bare $[0,1,2]$:
reading the compact copy at $[2,0,1]$ walks disk rows $[9,2,5]$, the original
selected order, exactly the order the disk branch keeps.  The training loop
applies its shared epoch permutation to whichever index the branch returned,
so it trains the same cosmology at the same step whether host memory chose the
resident or the disk branch.  The \code{stage\_source} routine refuses a
selection with a repeated row, since the seeded selection is a unique
permutation prefix and a repeat is upstream corruption.  The \code{stage-ram}
gate drives the real per-source loader in both storage regimes, draws one
shared epoch permutation, and compares the executed parameters, targets, and
minibatch membership and order row for row, with a mutation arm restoring the
bare $[0,1,2]$ that must fail.

\subsection{Worked memory calculation}

Assume $n_u=250{,}000$, $P=20$, $D=3{,}000$, and both arrays are stored as
\code{float32}.  One element occupies four bytes.  The resident copies need
\begin{align*}
  B_C &= 250{,}000\times20\times4
       =20{,}000{,}000\ \text{bytes},\\
  B_V &= 250{,}000\times3{,}000\times4
       =3{,}000{,}000{,}000\ \text{bytes},\\
  B_I &= 250{,}000\times8
       =2{,}000{,}000\ \text{bytes},\\
  B_{\rm total}&=B_C+B_V+B_I=3{,}022{,}000{,}000\ \text{bytes}.
\end{align*}
$B_I$ is the local index array.  Its entries use NumPy's 64-bit integer
dtype, so each one occupies eight bytes.  The decision compares this
three-term total with the configured host-memory allowance.  The status line
names all three terms---parameters, data vector, and index---prints their
exact total, the comparison operator that held, the budget, and the selected
branch, so the displayed arithmetic sums.  The comparison is strict
($B_{\rm total}<B_{\rm budget}$): an exact fill streams from disk, keeping
transient working room above the copies' own bytes, and the gate pins the
below, equal, and above cases so this equality policy cannot drift.

\subsection{What has not happened yet}

At the end of \code{stage\_train} and \code{stage\_val}, the program has
selected and located the rows.  It has not yet built a neural network, an
optimizer, or a covariance-weighted loss.  Apart from the seeded permutation,
the arrays are still NumPy objects on the host.

The next method, \code{build\_geometry}, creates two maps:
\begin{widetext}
\begin{align*}
  \text{parameter geometry: }&
  \btheta\longmapsto x,
  &&\text{raw cosmology to centered, rescaled network input},\\
  \text{output geometry: }&
  \bd\longmapsto t,
  &&\text{physical data vector to centered, covariance-scaled target}.
\end{align*}
\end{widetext}
Only after those maps exist can the batching code convert a NumPy batch with
\code{torch.from\_numpy}, cast it to the model's \code{float32} dtype, move it
to the selected device, and encode it.  That is the subject of
Sec.~\ref{chap:geometries}.

\subsection{Section summary}

The first numerical job is not neural-network training.  It is identity
preservation.  The staging code must keep four statements true:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item Parameter row $i$ and data-vector row $i$ describe the same
        cosmology.
  \item Parameter names and column order match the covariance that will
        transform them.
  \item The seeded subset is selected after the declared physical cuts and
        contains the requested absolute number of rows.
  \item Every row index is interpreted in the coordinate system of the array
        it indexes: compact local rows for a resident copy, original global
        rows for a file-backed dump.
\end{enumerate}
Once those conditions hold, the program has a staged training source.  The
next problem is numerical geometry: how to transform correlated parameters
and correlated outputs into coordinates a neural network can learn without
silently changing the $\chi^2$ that defines emulator accuracy.

\section{Geometries: changing coordinates without changing the science}
\label{chap:geometries}

The staged source contains physical numbers.  The columns can differ by many
orders of magnitude and can be strongly correlated.  A neural network trains
more easily when its inputs and targets have comparable scales.  The geometry
classes own that coordinate change.

Here, \emph{geometry} does not mean spacetime geometry.  It means a reversible
map between physical coordinates and network coordinates.  There are two
maps:
\begin{equation*}
  \btheta \xleftrightarrow[\text{decode}]{\text{encode}} x,
  \qquad
  \bd \xleftrightarrow[\text{decode}]{\text{encode}} t.
\end{equation*}
$x$ is the tensor presented to the model.  $t$ is the tensor the model is
trained to predict.  The inverse maps are part of the saved artifact because
an emulator prediction is useful only after it has returned to physical
units.

\subsection{The parameter geometry}

The ordinary input transform is \code{ParamGeometry}.  The constructor stores
four facts:
\begin{table*}[t]
\centering
\begin{tabular}{p{0.20\textwidth}p{0.68\textwidth}}
\toprule
Field & Meaning \\
\midrule
\code{names} & Parameter names in the exact column order of the covariance. \\
\code{center} & Training-set mean $\boldsymbol\mu$. \\
\code{evecs} & Orthonormal covariance eigenvectors $Q$. \\
\code{sqrt\_ev} & Square roots of the covariance eigenvalues,
                   $s_j=\sqrt{\lambda_j}$. \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\caption{Persisted fields of the ordinary parameter geometry.  Together they
define both directions of the reversible map in
Eqs.~(\ref{eq:param-encode})--(\ref{eq:param-decode}); none may be inferred
later from a current default or filename.}
\label{tab:param-geometry-fields}
\end{table*}

The covariance is symmetric, so NumPy computes
\begin{equation}
  \Sigma = Q\,\mathrm{diag}(\lambda_1,\ldots,\lambda_P)Q^{\mathsf T}.
\end{equation}
For a batch of raw parameter rows $\Theta\in\mathbb{R}^{B\times P}$,
encoding is
\begin{equation}
  X = (\Theta-\boldsymbol\mu)Q\,\mathrm{diag}(s_1^{-1},\ldots,s_P^{-1}).
  \label{eq:param-encode}
\end{equation}
Subtracting the mean centers the cloud at zero.  Multiplication by $Q$ rotates
the correlated ellipsoid onto its principal axes.  Division by $s_j$ gives
each rotated direction unit scale.  This complete operation is called
\emph{whitening}.

Decoding performs the inverse operations in reverse order:
\begin{equation}
  \Theta =
  \left[X\,\mathrm{diag}(s_1,\ldots,s_P)\right]Q^{\mathsf T}
  +\boldsymbol\mu.
  \label{eq:param-decode}
\end{equation}
Because $Q$ is orthonormal, $Q Q^{\mathsf T}=I$.  A numerical identity test
must therefore verify
\begin{equation}
  \code{decode(encode(theta))}\simeq\code{theta}
\end{equation}
over representative rows, with an error consistent with the stored
\code{float32} representation.

\subsubsection{Worked whitening example with two parameters}

Consider two input parameters with different physical units: an amplitude
parameter measured in dimensionless units and an expansion rate measured in
${\rm km}\,{\rm s}^{-1}{\rm Mpc}^{-1}$.  Suppose their training-set mean is
\begin{equation*}
  \boldsymbol\mu=(0.30,70.0),
\end{equation*}
and the covariance eigenvectors and square-root eigenvalues are
\begin{equation*}
 Q=\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}
 \begin{pmatrix}1&1\\-1&1\end{pmatrix},
 \qquad
 (s_1,s_2)=(1,3).
\end{equation*}
The numerical values are illustrative; the program obtains these arrays from
the actual training covariance.

Take one raw cosmology
\begin{equation*}
  \boldsymbol\theta=(0.32,73.0).
\end{equation*}
Encoding first subtracts the mean.  The offset row is
$(0.02,3.0)$.  It then multiplies that row on the right by $Q$:
\begin{align*}
 (0.02,3.0)Q
 &=\left(\frac{0.02-3.0}{\sqrt{2}},
          \frac{0.02+3.0}{\sqrt{2}}\right)\\
 &\simeq(-2.107,2.135).
\end{align*}
This multiplication forms two new coordinates.  Each new coordinate is a
linear combination of both raw parameters; it is not ``the normalized first
column'' or ``the normalized second column.''  Finally, division by
$(s_1,s_2)$ gives the network input
\begin{equation*}
  x\simeq(-2.107,0.712).
\end{equation*}
The encoded numbers are dimensionless.  The eigenvectors combine the original
units only after the covariance has supplied the corresponding conversion and
scale information.

Decoding uses the stored operations in reverse.  Multiplying by the scales
gives $(-2.107,2.135)$.  Right-multiplying by $Q^{\mathsf T}$ returns the raw
offset $(0.02,3.0)$, and adding $\boldsymbol\mu$ returns $(0.32,73.0)$.  A
decoder that applies $Q$ instead of $Q^{\mathsf T}$ would have the correct
shape and the wrong cosmology.  This is why the identity test compares values,
not only dimensions.

For a batch, the same operations act on every row.  NumPy and PyTorch
broadcast the one-dimensional mean across the batch dimension: a stored
mean with shape $(P,)$ is subtracted from an input with shape $(B,P)$ as if
the same mean row had been written $B$ times.  Broadcasting does not allocate
those repeated rows.  The matrix multiply then maps all $B$ rows in one
operation, but its mathematical meaning is still the row-by-row calculation
shown above.

\paragraph{Alternative constructors.}
The method \code{from\_covmat} reads and diagonalizes the training
covariance.  The method \code{from\_state} rebuilds the object from saved
arrays.  The ordinary \code{\_\_init\_\_} method only stores fields.  Keeping
those jobs separate means inference never needs the original covariance file.

\subsection{Views, copies, dtype, and device}

The geometry converts each array with
\begin{verbatim}
torch.as_tensor(array,
                dtype=torch.float32,
                device=device)
\end{verbatim}
If the source already has the requested dtype and device, PyTorch may reuse
its storage.  If dtype or device changes, it creates new storage.  Moving from
a NumPy CPU array to CUDA necessarily copies the values to GPU memory.

The model uses \code{float32}.  Covariance construction and diagnostic
reductions often use \code{float64} on the CPU because sums and
eigendecompositions lose more information than a neural-network forward pass.
The program must state where a cast occurs.  A finite nonzero scale in
\code{float64} is not automatically usable if it becomes zero in
\code{float32}.

\subsection{The data-vector geometry}

Cosmic-shear output uses \code{DataVectorGeometry}.  Its first operation is a
mask.  The full physical vector has width $D$, but the likelihood keeps only
$K$ entries.  \code{dest\_idx} stores their global column positions.

For a batch $V\in\mathbb{R}^{B\times D}$,
\begin{equation*}
  V_{\rm keep}=V[:,\code{dest\_idx}]
  \in\mathbb{R}^{B\times K}.
\end{equation*}
This is PyTorch advanced indexing.  It gathers the selected columns in the
specified order and returns a new tensor.  The method is named
\code{squeeze}, but it is not \code{torch.squeeze}: no size-one dimension is
removed.

The inverse method, \code{unsqueeze}, allocates a zero-filled
$B\times D$ tensor and assigns the $K$ values back into
\code{dest\_idx}.  Masked locations remain zero.  This is a scatter
operation.

After masking and centering, the dense-covariance geometry applies the same
eigenvector construction as Eq.~\eqref{eq:param-encode}.  If
\begin{equation}
  \Sigma_{\rm keep}=U\,\mathrm{diag}(\eta)U^{\mathsf T},
\end{equation}
then a centered kept vector $r$ is whitened as
\begin{equation}
  t=rU\,\mathrm{diag}(\eta^{-1/2}).
\end{equation}
The ordinary squared length of $t$ equals the covariance-weighted physical
length of $r$:
\begin{equation}
  \lVert t\rVert^2 = r \Sigma_{\rm keep}^{-1} r^{\mathsf T}.
  \label{eq:whiten-chi2}
\end{equation}
This identity is why training in whitened space does not redefine the
scientific metric.

For example, let a two-coordinate physical residual be $r=(0.5,3.0)$ and let
the covariance be diagonal with variances $(0.25,9.0)$.  Its inverse is
$\Sigma_{\rm keep}^{-1}=\mathrm{diag}(4,1/9)$, so
\begin{equation*}
 r\Sigma_{\rm keep}^{-1}r^{\mathsf T}
 =0.5^2\times4+3.0^2\times\frac{1}{9}=2.
\end{equation*}
Whitening divides the two residuals by their standard deviations $(0.5,3)$,
which gives $t=(1,1)$.  The ordinary squared length is again
$1^2+1^2=2$.  The direct contraction and the whitened sum use different code
paths to express the same scientific distance; comparing them gives an
independent check of the geometry.

\subsection{Diagonal output families}

Some families have one independent scale per output instead of a dense
covariance rotation.  They use
\begin{equation}
  t_k=\frac{d_k-\bar d_k}{\sigma_k},
  \qquad
  d_k=t_k\sigma_k+\bar d_k.
\end{equation}

\begin{table*}[t]
\centering
\begin{tabular}{p{0.19\textwidth}p{0.28\textwidth}p{0.43\textwidth}}
\toprule
Geometry & Coordinate axis & Stored physical facts \\
\midrule
\code{ScalarGeometry} & Named derived parameters & Output names, center,
scale. \\
CMB diagonal & Multipole $\ell$ & Spectrum, $\ell$, units,
fiducial spectrum, cosmic-variance scale, amplitude law. \\
\code{GridGeometry} & Redshift $z$ & Quantity, units, target law, offset,
stored $z$ grid. \\
\code{Grid2DGeometry} & Flattened $(z,k)$ surface & Quantity, units, target
law, both axes, constant-point mask. \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\caption{Output families whose standardization is diagonal in their stored
coordinate order.  The axis column defines what output index $k$ means; the
facts in the last column travel with the artifact so an equal-width but
different physical axis is refused.}
\label{tab:diagonal-geometries}
\end{table*}

The axes and units are artifact facts.  A consumer reads them from the saved
geometry rather than reconstructing them from a filename or a current code
default.

\subsection{The geometry acceptance test}

A geometry is ready only when four numerical statements hold:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item Every scale is finite and representably nonzero in its stored dtype.
  \item \code{decode(encode(raw))} returns the raw value within the declared
        floating-point tolerance.
  \item Saving and rebuilding the state leaves the transform unchanged.
  \item The squared whitened residual agrees with a direct physical
        covariance contraction.
\end{enumerate}

These four statements are the acceptance contract, not a claim that every
current constructor already enforces them.

\paragraph{Current gap.}
Three live construction paths can violate the first statement before a later
round-trip is attempted.  One covariance constructor takes a square root of a
numerically negative eigenvalue without first rejecting it, producing NaN.
A block-diagonal path clips a negative eigenvalue to zero and later divides by
the resulting zero scale.  Diagonal families can also accept a positive
\code{float64} scale that underflows to exact zero when stored as
\code{float32}.  A finite check before the storage-dtype conversion does not
cover that last case.

\paragraph{Required closure.}
Every geometry constructor and \code{from\_state} readback applies one shared
post-conversion check: centers, factors, and scales are finite, and every
divisor is representably nonzero in the dtype that encode will use.
Covariance paths also refuse a non-positive spectrum with a typed explanation
instead of clipping it into an irreversible map.  Identity gates exercise the
real constructors, storage conversion, encode/decode round trip, and direct
physical-score equality.

\section{The neural designs and their constructible specifications}
\label{chap:models}

Once the geometries exist, the program knows the model's input width and
output width.  It can construct a neural network without embedding data-file
knowledge in the network class.

\subsection{Define each design before comparing it}

A \emph{design} is the rule for connecting trainable operations, fixed
coordinate operations, and any analytic physics component into one predictor.
The names used by this library mean:

\begin{table*}[t]
\centering
\begin{tabular}{p{0.16\textwidth}p{0.29\textwidth}p{0.45\textwidth}}
\toprule
Design & Definition & What is learned and what is fixed \\
\midrule
ResMLP & A residual multilayer perceptron: dense layers act on the complete
feature vector, and each block adds a learned correction to its input. & All
dense weights and activation parameters are learned; input/output geometries
are fixed after fitting. \\
ResCNN & A residual model with a one-dimensional convolutional correction
head.  A convolution reuses one local kernel along an ordered coordinate. &
The trunk, kernels, and head gate are learned; scatter/gather coordinates and
validity masks are fixed. \\
ResTRF & A residual model with a transformer correction head.  A transformer
repeats attention, feature mixing, and residual additions so each token can
use information from other tokens. & Query/key/value projections, mixing
layers, and head gate are learned; token/coordinate layout is fixed. \\
IA template model & A factored intrinsic-alignment model.  Intrinsic alignment
means galaxy shapes correlated by local structure rather than gravitational
lensing.  The network predicts basis templates and a closed-form law combines
them with named IA amplitudes. & Template values are learned; the amplitude
combination law is fixed physics. \\
PCE plus neural residual & A polynomial-chaos expansion supplies a frozen
smooth base; a neural network learns what that finite polynomial basis misses.
& Selected polynomial terms and coefficients are fitted once, then frozen;
the residual network remains trainable. \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\caption{Constructible predictor designs.  A design specifies tensor
connectivity and the boundary between learned and fixed operations; it does
not choose the data files, physical units, optimizer, or output geometry.}
\label{tab:model-designs}
\end{table*}

A multilayer perceptron, abbreviated MLP, is a sequence of affine maps and
nonlinear activations.  A dense or fully connected affine map lets every
output feature depend on every input feature.  ``Residual'' means a learned
correction is added to an unchanged skip path; it does not mean a separate
physical residual unless the loss explicitly constructs one.  The following
subsections define the mechanics behind each row.

\subsection{A class is passed as data}

The model configuration is a dictionary whose \code{cls} entry holds a class
object and whose remaining entries are constructor arguments:
\begin{verbatim}
model_opts = {"cls":         ResMLP,
              "int_dim_res": 256,
              "n_blocks":    4,
              "block_opts":  block_opts}
\end{verbatim}
A class object is callable.  Calling \code{ResMLP(...)} creates a new module.
\code{make\_model} removes the bookkeeping entries, injects the data-dependent
input and output dimensions, constructs the module, and moves it to the
selected device.

The same pattern constructs the optimizer and scheduler.  Computed values
such as batch-scaled learning rate and device-dependent fused behavior are
injected by the factory instead of being stored as stale configuration facts.

\subsection{Factories create distinct submodules}

Residual blocks receive activation and normalization factories.  A factory is
a callable such as \code{act(width)} that returns a new \code{nn.Module}.
Calling it once per layer gives every layer its own learnable activation
parameters.  Passing one already-created activation instance would share its
parameters across layers.

Parameter-free activations do not need the width, but they still obey the
same interface:
\begin{verbatim}
def relu_factory(dim):
    return nn.ReLU()
\end{verbatim}
The argument is intentionally unused.  The uniform interface lets the model
construction loop treat learnable and fixed activations identically.

\subsection{Registration: why ModuleList matters}

PyTorch discovers trainable parameters by walking registered child modules.
\code{nn.Sequential} and \code{nn.ModuleList} register their contents.  A bare
Python list does not.  A plain list is safe only as a temporary builder that
is immediately expanded into \code{nn.Sequential(*layers)} or copied into
\code{nn.ModuleList(layers)}.

Registration controls four operations:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item inclusion in \code{model.parameters()},
  \item movement by \code{model.to(device)},
  \item inclusion in \code{state\_dict()}, and
  \item switching between training and evaluation modes.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{ResMLP: the baseline map}

Table~\ref{tab:resmlp-shapes} separates the two rectangular projections from
the width-preserving trunk.  A dense layer does not automatically preserve
dimension: \code{nn.Linear(in\_features,out\_features)} returns exactly
\code{out\_features} values.  Only the dense layers inside \code{ResBlock}
are required to be square.

\begin{table*}[t]
\centering
\begin{tabular}{p{0.17\textwidth}p{0.18\textwidth}p{0.30\textwidth}
                p{0.16\textwidth}p{0.10\textwidth}}
\toprule
Stage & Input shape & Operation & Output shape & Inside a
\code{ResBlock}? \\
\midrule
Entry projection & $(B,P_{\rm enc})$
  & \code{Linear(P\_enc,W)} mixes the encoded parameters and changes the
    feature width.
  & $(B,W)$ & No \\
Residual trunk & $(B,W)$
  & Apply $N_{\rm block}$ independent residual blocks.  Every internal dense
    layer is \code{Linear(W,W)}.
  & $(B,W)$ & Yes \\
Exit projection & $(B,W)$
  & \code{Linear(W,K)} changes hidden features into the $K$ encoded target
    coordinates.
  & $(B,K)$ & No \\
Final affine & $(B,K)$
  & Multiply by one learned gain and add one learned bias.  Broadcasting
    preserves the target shape.
  & $(B,K)$ & No \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\caption{Shape contract of the baseline \code{ResMLP}.  The rectangular entry
and exit projections change feature count.  A residual block begins only
after the entry projection has established width $W$, and it ends before the
exit projection changes $W$ to $K$.}
\label{tab:resmlp-shapes}
\end{table*}

For a batch of $B=256$ cosmologies, $P_{\rm enc}=12$, $W=128$, and $K=780$,
the entry projection reads a \shape{(256,12)} tensor and returns
\shape{(256,128)}.  Every residual block reads and returns
\shape{(256,128)}.  The exit projection alone produces
\shape{(256,780)}.  Calling the entry projection a residual-block layer would
erase this defining equal-width condition.

The library's residual block is not the bare formula $h+F(h)$ followed
immediately by return.  It contains $L$ internal dense layers.  The skip is
added to the final dense layer's output; that sum then passes through the
final normalization and activation.  Figure~\ref{fig:resblock} and the
recurrence below state the exact order.

\subsection{Structured correction heads}

\code{ResCNN} and \code{ResTRF} retain the ResMLP trunk and add a correction
head.  The trunk predicts every kept output.  The head reshapes that output
into physical bins, learns local or cross-bin structure, and adds a gated
correction:
\begin{equation}
  \widehat T = T_{\rm trunk} + g\,T_{\rm correction}.
\end{equation}

The convolutional head treats tomographic bins as channels and angular
positions as the one-dimensional coordinate.  The transformer head treats
bins as tokens.  A token is one row presented to attention; attention mixes
information between token rows.

Ragged bins have different lengths.  The implementation scatters them into a
rectangle of width equal to the longest bin.  Padding positions require an
explicit validity mask because convolution, affine modulation, and attention
can turn an initial zero into a nonzero hidden value.

The correction is initialized to zero, so the initial structured model equals
its trunk.  A nonzero outer gate lets gradients reach the zero-initialized
last correction layer on the first optimizer step.  An activation placed
after a zero-initialized layer must have a nonzero derivative at zero, or the
head can remain permanently asleep.

\subsection{Factored intrinsic-alignment designs}

The factored models do not ask the network to learn known polynomial
dependence on intrinsic-alignment amplitudes.  The input geometry appends the
raw amplitudes after the whitened non-amplitude parameters.  The model reads
only the non-amplitude block and emits $T_{\rm tmpl}$ templates:
\begin{equation*}
  X \longmapsto
  \widehat T_{\rm tmpl}
  \in\mathbb{R}^{B\times T_{\rm tmpl}\times K}.
\end{equation*}
For a fixed template index $j$, the slice
$\widehat T_{\rm tmpl}[:,j,:]$ has shape $(B,K)$: it contains that template
for every cosmology in the minibatch.  The symbol $T_{\rm tmpl}$ counts
templates and does not denote the encoded target tensor $T$.
The loss reads the appended amplitudes and combines the templates with the
known coefficients.  This separation keeps the exact amplitude law outside
the neural approximation.

\subsection{One dense layer, written without shorthand}

The basic learned operation is an affine map.  For a minibatch
$H\in\mathbb{R}^{B\times W}$, a PyTorch \code{nn.Linear(W,W)} computes
\begin{equation}
  Z_{bi}=\sum_{j=0}^{W-1}H_{bj}A_{ij}+a_i.
  \label{eq:linear}
\end{equation}
$A_{ij}$ is a learnable weight and $a_i$ is a learnable bias.  The letter
$W$ denotes the hidden width, whereas $A$ denotes the weight matrix.  The first
index $b$ selects a cosmology in the minibatch.  The index $j$ selects an
incoming feature, and $i$ selects an outgoing feature.  PyTorch stores the
weight matrix with shape \shape{(out\_features,in\_features)} and applies the
transpose convention needed for a batch whose feature axis is last.

An affine map alone cannot represent a general nonlinear physical response.
Composing two affine maps still gives one affine map.  The activation is
therefore applied element by element after selected linear layers:
\begin{equation*}
  H'_{bi}=\phi(Z_{bi}).
\end{equation*}
Element by element means that the activation changes each scalar without
mixing feature columns.  The linear layer performs the mixing; the activation
provides the bend.

The entry and exit projections are rectangular.  If the encoded cosmology
has $P_{\rm enc}=12$ features, the hidden width is $W=128$, and the target has
$K=780$ values, their shapes are
\begin{equation*}
  \shape{(128,12)},\qquad \shape{(780,128)}.
\end{equation*}
All dense layers between them are square \shape{(128,128)}.  This fixed width
lets a residual block add its input to its correction without another shape
adapter.

\subsection{A residual block, operation by operation}

\begin{figure*}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.96\textwidth]
  {texnotes/figures/fig05_residual_block.pdf}
\caption{The exact residual-block order used by the library.  Every dense
layer inside the dashed boundary maps width $W$ to the same width $W$.  The
skip is added after the final internal dense layer and before that layer's
normalization and activation.  The rectangular model-entry and model-exit
projections change dimensions, but they are not part of a residual block.}
\label{fig:resblock}
\end{figure*}

Figure~\ref{fig:resblock} names the two paths.  Let $h_0$ be the block input,
and let the block contain $L$ internal dense layers.  For every nonfinal layer
$i=1,\ldots,L-1$, the executed recurrence is
\begin{align}
 z_i &= h_{i-1}A_i^{\mathsf T}+a_i,\nonumber\\
 h_i &= \phi_i\!\left(N_i(z_i)\right).
 \label{eq:resblock-interior}
\end{align}
$A_i$ is a square $W\times W$ weight matrix, $a_i$ is a length-$W$ bias,
$N_i$ is that layer's independently constructed normalization module, and
$\phi_i$ is that layer's independently constructed activation module.  Every
array in Eq.~\eqref{eq:resblock-interior} has shape $(B,W)$ after the batch
matrix multiplication.  The length-$W$ bias is broadcast across the $B$
minibatch rows; PyTorch does not store $B$ separate copies of it.

The last layer has a different order:
\begin{align}
 z_L &= h_{L-1}A_L^{\mathsf T}+a_L,\nonumber\\
 s_L &= z_L+h_0,\nonumber\\
 h_{\rm out} &= \phi_L\!\left(N_L(s_L)\right).
 \label{eq:resblock-final}
\end{align}
The addition therefore lands after the final internal dense layer.  With the
default $L=2$, it lands after the second dense layer.  It lands before the
second layer's normalization and activation.  The result returned by the
library is $\phi_L(N_L(z_L+h_0))$; describing it as a bare $h_0+F(h_0)$ would
omit two executed operations.

The skip path bypasses the internal dense calculation that forms $z_L$.
Backward differentiation still passes through the final $N_L$ and $\phi_L$
because they follow the addition.  This is a shorter gradient route, not a
claim that the complete block derivative contains an untouched identity from
output to input.

The sum is coordinate by coordinate.  Entry $(b,j)$ of $z_L$ is added only to
entry $(b,j)$ of $h_0$.  Concatenation would append feature columns and return
shape $(B,2W)$; addition returns $(B,W)$.  This is why every dense layer inside
the block must preserve width.  A rectangular $P_{\rm enc}\to W$ or $W\to K$
projection belongs outside the block.

The input is not modified in place.  Operations construct new tensors whose
autograd records point back to their parents.  \emph{Autograd} is PyTorch's
automatic-differentiation system: it records the executed tensor operations
and later applies the chain rule in reverse during \code{backward()}.
Avoiding an in-place overwrite keeps the original value available to the skip
addition and to backward differentiation.

\subsection{Normalization controls the activation's operating range}

The model's normalization slot is an affine rescaling, not batch
normalization.  In its shared form,
\begin{equation*}
  \widetilde Z = gZ+b,
\end{equation*}
where $g$ and $b$ are learned scalars.  In its per-feature form, $g_i$ and
$b_i$ are vectors of length $W$ and are broadcast across the batch axis.
\emph{Broadcasting} means that PyTorch treats the missing batch dimension as
repeated without allocating $B$ copies of the vector.

The purpose is to keep values in the region where the activation derivative
is useful.  If a saturating activation receives extremely large magnitudes,
its output becomes nearly constant and its derivative approaches zero.  A
zero derivative prevents upstream weights from receiving a learning signal.
The affine normalization learns a scale and shift that can return those
features to the curved portion of the activation.

No statistic of the current minibatch is used.  Consequently, prediction for
one cosmology does not depend on which other cosmologies happen to share its
batch.  The same learned $g$ and $b$ are used during training and inference,
and the module has no running mean or variance buffer to synchronize with an
exponential moving average.

\subsection{Activation factories and learnable activation parameters}

The activation configuration names a family.  A factory creates one module
per activation site.  In the default smooth family, the module owns learned
shape parameters such as a left-hand slope and a transition sharpness.  They
are ordinary \code{nn.Parameter} objects: they appear in
\code{model.parameters()}, receive gradients, move with
\code{model.to(device)}, and are saved in the state dictionary.

A \emph{state dictionary} is a mapping from stable string names to tensors.
It contains parameters and registered buffers, not Python source code.
Rebuilding therefore creates the architecture first and then loads the
recorded tensors into matching names and shapes.

An activation used immediately after an exactly zero-initialized correction
layer needs special scrutiny.  At initialization its input is exactly zero.
If $\phi'(0)=0$, the chain rule multiplies the correction layer's gradient by
zero and that path cannot wake on the first update.  The structured-head
schema therefore considers both $\phi(0)$, which controls exact identity at
initialization, and $\phi'(0)$, which controls whether the head can learn.

\subsection{The convolutional correction head}

Cosmic-shear outputs are not an arbitrary flat vector.  Values are grouped by
correlation type and tomographic-bin pair, and each group varies along angular
separation.  The convolutional head first changes layout without changing
values:
\begin{equation*}
 \shape{(B,K)}
 \longrightarrow
 \shape{(B,N_{\rm bin},N_\theta)}.
\end{equation*}
The first operation is a fixed coordinate scatter.  A scatter writes each
kept output into a declared rectangle position.  It is not a learned layer.

\begin{figure*}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.94\textwidth]
  {texnotes/figures/fig06_cnn_head.pdf}
\caption{The convolutional correction-head path.  Reshaping and
scatter/gather operations are fixed coordinate transformations.  Only the
convolution blocks and outer gate contain learned values.}
\label{fig:cnn-head}
\end{figure*}

A one-dimensional convolution slides the same kernel along the angular axis.
For kernel half-width $q$, an output at angle index $u$ has the schematic form
\begin{equation*}
 y_{c,u}=\sum_{c'}\sum_{s=-q}^{q}
          k_{c,c',s}\,x_{c',u+s}+a_c.
\end{equation*}
The index $c'$ ranges over input bins, so the layer can mix bin pairs at
nearby angular positions.  Reusing $k$ at every $u$ is weight sharing: the
number of learned values does not grow with the number of angular positions.

Bins can be ragged, meaning that they contain different numbers of valid
angles.  They are stored in a rectangle by padding shorter rows.  The validity
mask marks real positions.  Zeroing once at the input is insufficient because
a convolutional bias, cross-bin mixing, or feature-wise modulation can make a
padded cell nonzero.  The mask must be applied wherever the architecture can
repopulate invalid cells and again before gathering the correction.

\subsection{The transformer correction head}

The transformer head uses the same physical rectangle but a different mixing
mechanism.  Each bin is a token; a token is one row presented to attention.
Attention constructs query, key, and value representations:
\begin{equation*}
 Q_{\rm qry}=XA_Q,\qquad
 K_{\rm key}=XA_K,\qquad
 V_{\rm val}=XA_V,
\end{equation*}
then computes
\begin{equation}
 \operatorname{Attention}(X)
 =\operatorname{softmax}\!\left(
   \frac{Q_{\rm qry}K_{\rm key}^{\mathsf T}}{\sqrt{d_h}}
  \right)V_{\rm val}.
 \label{eq:attention}
\end{equation}
The softmax converts each row of similarity scores into nonnegative weights
that sum to one.  Unlike a convolution, attention can couple distant bins in
one layer.

For one attention head, let $B$ be batch size, $S$ the number of tokens, and
$d_h$ the head-feature width.  Then $X$ has shape $(B,S,d_{\rm model})$;
each projected tensor $Q_{\rm qry}$, $K_{\rm key}$, and $V_{\rm val}$ has
shape $(B,S,d_h)$.  The matrix product with the last two axes transposed has
shape $(B,S,S)$: entry $(b,s,u)$ scores how strongly token $s$ reads token
$u$ in sample $b$.  Multiplication by $V_{\rm val}$ returns
$(B,S,d_h)$.  With $B=2$, $S=3$, and $d_h=4$, the score tensor is therefore
$(2,3,3)$ rather than $(2,4,4)$.  Multiple heads repeat this calculation with
separate projections and concatenate their feature axes before an output
projection.

The layout here is not the usual natural-language layout.  Angular positions
are feature coordinates inside each bin token.  A textbook token mask alone
therefore cannot hide every ragged angular feature; the implementation needs
the same per-block coordinate-validity mask used by the scatter.  The mask's
shape and meaning are persisted with the artifact because reconstructing it
from counts can assign the right number of cells to the wrong coordinates.

\subsection{FiLM: conditioning a head on the cosmology}

Feature-wise linear modulation, abbreviated FiLM, lets a correction head
depend directly on the encoded cosmology.  A small parameter network computes
a scale $\gamma(x)$ and shift $\beta(x)$, then applies
\begin{equation*}
  H'=\gamma(x)\odot H+\beta(x).
\end{equation*}
$\odot$ denotes elementwise multiplication.  The scale and shift are
broadcast over the declared spatial axis.  FiLM is initialized as the
identity, $\gamma=1$ and $\beta=0$, so adding the option does not perturb the
initial trunk prediction.

For example, suppose the head activation $H$ has shape $(B,C,S)$: $C$ is a
feature channel and $S$ is a spatial or angular coordinate.  If the parameter
network emits $\gamma(x)$ and $\beta(x)$ with shape $(B,C)$, the code inserts
a singleton final axis to obtain $(B,C,1)$.  Broadcasting reuses each
sample-and-channel value across all $S$ coordinates without materializing
$S$ stored copies.  It must not reuse a value across the batch axis: each
cosmology has its own modulation.

The ownership distinction is useful: the main trunk learns the full
cosmology-to-output map, the convolution or transformer learns structured
corrections, and FiLM tells that correction which cosmology it is correcting.
Those roles should remain visible in tensor names and artifact metadata.

\section{Polynomial-chaos bases and neural residuals}
\label{sec:pce}

\subsection{Definition: polynomial, basis, expansion, and chaos}

A \emph{polynomial} in one variable is a finite sum of nonnegative integer
powers:
\begin{equation*}
 p(x)=a_0+a_1x+a_2x^2+\cdots+a_dx^d.
\end{equation*}
The numbers \(a_0,\ldots,a_d\) are coefficients and \(d\) is the degree when
\(a_d\ne0\).  A multivariate polynomial also contains products such as
\(x_1x_2\) or \(x_1^2x_3\).

A \emph{basis} is a set of building-block functions whose weighted sum can
represent the approximation.  This library uses Legendre polynomials on
\([-1,1]\):
\begin{align*}
 P_0(x)&=1,&
 P_1(x)&=x,\\
 P_2(x)&=\tfrac12(3x^2-1),&
 P_3(x)&=\tfrac12(5x^3-3x).
\end{align*}
Higher degrees follow the three-term recurrence
\begin{equation*}
 (n+1)P_{n+1}(x)
 =(2n+1)xP_n(x)-nP_{n-1}(x).
\end{equation*}
The input parameters are mapped from their fitted training box to
\([-1,1]\) before these curves are evaluated.

The fit receives already-whitened parameter columns $u_j$.  For each column,
it finds the selected-training minimum and maximum, then pads the half-width
by five percent:
\begin{align*}
 m_j&=\tfrac12(u_{j,\min}+u_{j,\max}),\\
 h_j&=1.05\,\tfrac12(u_{j,\max}-u_{j,\min})+10^{-12},\\
 \ell_j&=m_j-h_j,\qquad r_j=m_j+h_j,\\
 x_j&=2\frac{u_j-\ell_j}{r_j-\ell_j}-1.
\end{align*}
The $10^{-12}$ term prevents a zero denominator for a constant training
column.  If selected whitened values span $[-2,2]$, then
$(\ell,r)\simeq(-2.1,2.1)$ and $u=1.05$ maps to $x=0.5$.  These persisted
bounds define the basis coordinate.  The current inference-time clamp to the
box is a later domain policy and must not be confused with the affine map.

Legendre polynomials are \emph{orthogonal} under constant weight on that
interval:
\begin{equation*}
 \int_{-1}^{1}P_m(x)P_n(x)\,dx=0
 \quad\text{when }m\ne n.
\end{equation*}
Orthogonality means different basis directions do not duplicate one another
under this inner product.  If a uniformly distributed input defines the
probability measure \(dx/2\), then
\begin{equation*}
 \int_{-1}^{1}
 \left[\sqrt{2n+1}P_n(x)\right]^2\frac{dx}{2}=1.
\end{equation*}
This is why the implementation multiplies degree \(n\) by
\(\sqrt{2n+1}\).  Under the unnormalized measure \(dx\), the unit-norm factor
would instead be \(\sqrt{(2n+1)/2}\); naming the measure prevents a hidden
factor of two.

An \emph{expansion} is the weighted sum of selected basis terms.  The word
\emph{chaos} is historical terminology from representing functions of random
inputs with orthogonal polynomials; it does not mean that the emulator assumes
chaotic cosmological dynamics.  A polynomial chaos expansion, abbreviated
PCE, is therefore an orthogonal-polynomial approximation to the mapping from
input parameters to output quantities.

A multivariate PCE uses separable terms.  Separable means that one term is a
product of one-dimensional basis curves:
\begin{equation}
  \Psi_{\boldsymbol\alpha}(x)
  =\prod_{j=1}^{P}
  \sqrt{2\alpha_j+1}\,P_{\alpha_j}(x_j).
  \label{eq:pce-term}
\end{equation}
The multi-index $\boldsymbol\alpha=(\alpha_1,\ldots,\alpha_P)$ states which
degree is used for each parameter.  A total-degree rule keeps terms satisfying
$\sum_j\alpha_j\le p_{\max}$.  The implemented hybrid rule is
\begin{equation*}
 \left(\sum_j\alpha_j^q\right)^{1/q}\le p_{\max},
 \qquad
 \#\{j:\alpha_j\ne0\}\le r_{\max},
\end{equation*}
where $q\in(0,1]$ controls hyperbolic sparsity and $r_{\max}$ is the maximum
interaction order, meaning the number of distinct parameters allowed in one
product.  At $q=1$ the first rule is ordinary total degree.  With
$p_{\max}=4$ and $q=0.5$, $x_1^4$ scores $4$ and is kept, whereas
$x_1^2x_2^2$ scores $(\sqrt2+\sqrt2)^2=8$ and is excluded.  The rule expresses
a sparsity-of-effects assumption: concentrated degree is preferred over a
high-order interaction spread across many inputs.

The public \code{pce} block has eight controls.  They answer different
questions and therefore should not be collapsed into one generic ``size''
knob:
\begin{table*}[t]
\centering
\begin{tabular}{p{0.15\textwidth}p{0.20\textwidth}p{0.56\textwidth}}
\toprule
Configuration key & Default & Exact meaning \\
\midrule
\code{form} & required & Composition law.  \code{residual} adds the neural
correction to the base.  \code{ratio} uses the separately defined
multiplicative/gain construction and is legal only on compatible families. \\
\code{p\_max} & 4 & Hyperbolic degree bound on a candidate multi-index in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:pce-term}. \\
\code{r\_max} & 2 & Maximum number of distinct input parameters that may have
nonzero degree in one polynomial term. \\
\code{q} & 0.5 & Hyperbolic sparsity exponent in $(0,1]$; smaller values penalize
degrees spread across several inputs more strongly. \\
\code{k\_max} & 40 & Maximum number of leading singular-vector output modes
whose polynomial fits may be attempted.  It limits output directions, not
polynomial terms. \\
\code{loo\_max} & 0.05 & Largest accepted relative leave-one-out error for one
output mode.  A smaller value demands stronger generalization evidence. \\
\code{max\_terms} & 30 & Maximum number of active polynomial columns in the
greedy fit for one output mode.  The constant column counts as one active
term. \\
\code{max\_fail} & 4 & Stop visiting later singular-vector modes after this
many consecutive modes fail the leave-one-out threshold. \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\caption{The complete public PCE control block.  \code{p\_max},
\code{r\_max}, and \code{q} determine which polynomial columns exist;
\code{k\_max}, \code{loo\_max}, \code{max\_terms}, and \code{max\_fail}
control fitting and acceptance; \code{form} controls how the frozen base and
neural correction are composed.}
\label{tab:pce-fit-controls}
\end{table*}

\subsection{From one-dimensional curves to a term matrix}

For each box-mapped parameter \(x_j\), the basis builder evaluates
\(P_0(x_j),\ldots,P_{p_{\max}}(x_j)\).  Degree zero is the constant function.
A multi-index selects one degree from each parameter and multiplies those
values according to Eq.~\eqref{eq:pce-term}.

For two parameters and \(\boldsymbol\alpha=(2,1)\),
\begin{equation*}
 \Psi_{(2,1)}(x_1,x_2)
 =\sqrt5\,P_2(x_1)\sqrt3\,P_1(x_2).
\end{equation*}
This is an interaction term because both parameters affect it.  A total-degree
bound of two excludes the term since \(2+1=3\).

Allowed multi-indices are enumerated once in deterministic order.  That order
identifies coefficient rows and is persisted.  Rebuilding under a different
enumeration can preserve every matrix shape while assigning coefficients to
the wrong polynomials.

Number the candidate multi-indices
$\boldsymbol\alpha^{(0)},\ldots,\boldsymbol\alpha^{(M-1)}$.  The term matrix
$\Phi$ is defined entry by entry as
\begin{equation*}
 \Phi_{im}=\Psi_{\boldsymbol\alpha^{(m)}}(x_i).
\end{equation*}
It has shape \shape{(N,M)}: one row per cosmology and one column per candidate
polynomial term.  If $J$ output modes are accepted, their coefficient matrix
has shape \shape{(M,J)}.  The product has shape \shape{(N,J)} mode amplitudes;
multiplying by the stored $J$ mode vectors and adding the output mean produces
\shape{(N,K)} target predictions, where the global symbol $K$ again means
kept output coordinates.  The executed selector starts with the constant
column, repeatedly chooses the inactive column with largest normalized
correlation to the current residual, and refits ordinary least squares on the
entire growing active set.  This is a greedy orthogonal-matching-pursuit-like
procedure; the historical function name contains \code{lars}, but the code
does not execute the equiangular update that defines full LARS.  Selection
must terminate when every column is active;
re-appending an already-active column does not add a new model.

\subsubsection{A complete two-input degree-two PCE row}

Let $P=2$ mapped inputs and keep every total-degree-two term.  In deterministic
order the six multi-indices are
\begin{equation*}
\begin{aligned}
 &(0,0),(1,0),(2,0),\\
 &(0,1),(0,2),(1,1).
\end{aligned}
\end{equation*}
This is the implementation's persisted enumeration: it finishes the
single-variable degrees for input zero, then those for input one, then the
two-variable interaction.  Reordering the same set would attach coefficients
to different polynomials.
Choose the mapped point $x=(0.5,-0.5)$.  The one-dimensional values are
$P_1(0.5)=0.5$, $P_1(-0.5)=-0.5$, and
$P_2(\pm0.5)=-0.125$.  Including the normalization factors in
Eq.~\eqref{eq:pce-term}, the corresponding row of $\Phi$ is
\begin{equation*}
\begin{aligned}
 \Phi(x)\simeq(&1,\;0.8660,\;-0.2795,\\
                &-0.8660,\;-0.2795,\;-0.7500).
\end{aligned}
\end{equation*}
For a one-mode coefficient column
\begin{equation*}
 c=\bigl(1,\;0.2,\;-0.1,\;0.05,
          \;0.4,\;-0.2\bigr)^{\mathsf T},
\end{equation*}
the polynomial mode amplitude is the dot product
\begin{align*}
 \Phi(x)c
 &\simeq 1+0.1732+0.0280-0.0433-0.1118+0.1500\\
 &\simeq 1.1961.
\end{align*}
This is the entire PCE evaluation at one point: map the physical parameters to
$[-1,1]$, evaluate named basis terms, preserve their persisted column order,
and multiply by fitted coefficients.  The later SVD mode vectors turn one or
more such amplitudes back into the $K$ output coordinates.

\subsection{Fitting output modes, not target columns independently}

Let \(Y\in\mathbb{R}^{N\times K}\) be the whitened training targets.  The
mean $\bar Y$ is taken over the $N$ training rows separately for each output
coordinate, so it is mathematically a row vector of shape $(1,K)$; the stored
PyTorch buffer has shape $(K,)$ and broadcasts across rows.  It is not the mean
within each cosmology.  With $\mathbf 1_N$ denoting an $N$-element column of ones,
the centered matrix is $Y-\mathbf 1_N\bar Y$.  Its thin singular-value
decomposition is
\begin{equation*}
 Y-\mathbf 1_N\bar Y=USV^{\mathsf T}.
\end{equation*}
If $q\le\min(N,K)$ modes are retained by the factorization, $U$, $S$, and
$V$ have shapes $(N,q)$, $(q,q)$, and $(K,q)$.  SVD is a matrix
factorization.  Columns of \(V\) are orthogonal directions in output space;
the corresponding columns of \(US\) are one scalar amplitude per training
row.  Leading modes carry the largest target variance.

For mode \(j\), let
$z_j=(Y-\mathbf 1_N\bar Y)V_{:j}$, a length-$N$ column.  Sparse least squares
chooses a small set of PCE columns and coefficients \(c_j\) so that
\begin{equation*}
 \Phi c_j\approx z_j.
\end{equation*}
The implementation adds terms greedily and judges each candidate model by
leave-one-out error.  Conceptually, one row is omitted, the remaining rows are
fit, and the omitted row is predicted.  The prediction residual sum of
squares (PRESS) identity calculates these errors from the ordinary fit's
residual and leverage without performing \(N\) complete refits.  Leverage is
the diagonal of the fitted model's projection matrix; it measures how strongly
a row influences its own fitted value.

For a concrete four-row fit, suppose the ordinary fit has residuals
$e=(0.06,-0.04,0.02,-0.03)$ and projection diagonals
$h=(0.20,0.25,0.10,0.40)$.  The PRESS identity says the residual obtained by
leaving out row $i$ is $e_i/(1-h_{ii})$:
\begin{table*}[t]
\centering
\begin{tabular}{c@{\qquad}r@{\qquad}r@{\qquad}r}
\toprule
Omitted row & $e_i$ & $h_{ii}$ & leave-one-out residual \\
\midrule
0 & $0.06$ & $0.20$ & $0.0750$ \\
1 & $-0.04$ & $0.25$ & $-0.0533$ \\
2 & $0.02$ & $0.10$ & $0.0222$ \\
3 & $-0.03$ & $0.40$ & $-0.0500$ \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\caption{A four-row PRESS calculation.  Each final-column entry is the
residual that would be observed if the named row were omitted and the same
active polynomial columns were refitted.  PRESS obtains it from one ordinary
fit rather than performing four separate fits.}
\label{tab:press-example}
\end{table*}
Squaring and summing the final column gives the PRESS score.  A high-leverage
row has $h_{ii}$ near one, so its denominator is small and its omitted-row
error is amplified.  This calculation explains why the algorithm can score a
candidate term set without executing four separate fits in this example.

The threshold \code{loo\_max} receives a dimensionless relative mean-square
error,
\begin{equation*}
 \operatorname{LOO}_{\rm rel}
 =\frac{N^{-1}\sum_i[e_i/(1-h_{ii})]^2}
        {\operatorname{Var}(z_j)+10^{-30}},
\end{equation*}
where $\operatorname{Var}(z_j)$ is the population variance of the target mode
amplitudes over training rows.  For the four final-column values above, the
mean squared omitted-row residual is approximately $0.002866$.  If the mode
variance is $0.04$, the relative LOO is $0.002866/0.04\simeq0.0716$; a
threshold of $0.05$ rejects that mode.  The small denominator floor prevents
division by exact zero but does not turn a constant target into evidence of
generalization.

Only modes whose relative leave-one-out error is below \code{loo\_max} enter
the base.  Rejected modes are left for the neural refiner.  Reconstruction is
\begin{equation*}
 \widehat Y_{\rm PCE}
 =\mathbf 1_N\bar Y+\Phi C_{\rm mode}V_{\rm kept}^{\mathsf T}.
\end{equation*}
This mode-first design directs polynomial capacity toward the dominant smooth
directions of the covariance-whitened target.

\paragraph{Current gap.}
If every attempted mode misses \code{loo\_max}, the current fit nevertheless
force-keeps mode zero.  That fallback contradicts the threshold rule and can
insert a demonstrably poor base into the neural composition.

\paragraph{Required closure.}
The all-rejected state either constructs a clean no-base/refiner-only path or
follows an explicitly named and validated fallback policy.  It never labels a
threshold failure as an accepted polynomial mode merely to keep the coefficient
matrix nonempty.

Selection maintains a set of distinct active indices.  When every term is
active, no candidate remains and the loop terminates.  Filling unavailable
scores with a sentinel and applying unconditional \code{argmax} can otherwise
return column zero and append an already active term.

The basis is refit independently for every training-size sweep point.  Reusing
a fit from the largest point leaks rows into smaller points and invalidates
the learning-curve comparison.

The PCE may serve as a frozen base and the neural network may learn its
residual.  This composition is called neural PCE, abbreviated NPCE.  The word
``neural'' refers to the learned correction; the polynomial base itself is a
linear combination of fixed basis functions after fitting.  Write the neural
correction as \(r_{\boldsymbol\varphi}(x)\), where
\(\boldsymbol\varphi\) denotes trainable network parameters.  This notation is
deliberately different from the cosmological parameter vector
\(\btheta\).  In additive form,
\begin{equation*}
  \widehat t(x)=t_{\rm PCE}(x)+r_{\boldsymbol\varphi}(x).
\end{equation*}
In multiplicative form, the domain of the base and correction must make the
product meaningful.  The artifact records the allowed input domain and the
combination law.  Inference may clamp or refuse an out-of-domain point only
according to that persisted policy; an undocumented clamp silently changes
the mathematical model.

\subsection{Residual and ratio modes}

In residual mode, the neural target is
\begin{equation*}
 \delta(x)=t(x)-t_{\rm PCE}(x).
\end{equation*}
In ratio mode it is based on \(t/t_{\rm PCE}\) or the declared gain form, so
zeros and signs of the base require an explicit policy.  The code does not
silently switch to addition near a small denominator.

The loss reconstructs the complete prediction before computing physical
\(\Delta\chi^2\).  Scoring only correction coordinates can favor a base whose
scale makes the residual numerically small without improving the physical
prediction.

\subsection{Persistence and inference}

The artifact stores input-domain facts, basis family, ordered multi-indices,
selected terms, coefficient matrix, combination mode, and correction recipe.
The predictor evaluates base and correction on the same encoded input.

Out-of-domain behavior is named and persisted.  If clamping is the declared
approximation, its bounds are stored and reporting identifies a clamped
request.  If the model is valid only inside its training domain, prediction
raises outside it.  Coefficient values alone cannot reveal this policy.

\paragraph{Current gap.}
The current predictor clamps each mapped coordinate to \([-1,1]\) through an
unversioned implementation rule; the artifact does not yet persist a named
domain policy.  The preceding paragraph is the required closure, not a claim
that current artifacts already distinguish clamp from refusal.

\section{Learnable activation functions and their generalizations}
\label{sec:activations}

An activation function is the scalar nonlinear map placed between learned
linear layers.  Scalar means that the function is applied independently to
every feature value.  Learnable means that the curve itself contains
\code{nn.Parameter} tensors updated by the optimizer.  In this library every
hidden feature receives its own curve parameters, so two columns in the same
layer can learn different left-tail slopes, transition positions, or tail
exponents.

The implemented families form a hierarchy.  The baseline \(H\) has one
sigmoid transition and linear tails.  Multi-gate adds movable transitions in
the bulk.  Power-gated \(H\) gives the tails a bounded learned exponent.  The
gated-power family combines both extensions.

\subsection{The baseline \(H\): an identity--Swish interpolation}

Define the logistic sigmoid
\begin{equation*}
  \sigma(z)=\frac{1}{1+\exp(-z)}.
\end{equation*}
Its value lies strictly between zero and one.  The baseline activation is
\begin{equation}
 H(x)=\left[\gamma+(1-\gamma)\sigma(\beta x)\right]x.
 \label{eq:H}
\end{equation}
All operations are elementwise when \(x\), \(\gamma\), and \(\beta\) are
feature vectors.  Equation~\eqref{eq:H} can also be written
\begin{equation*}
 H(x)=\gamma x+(1-\gamma)\,x\sigma(\beta x).
\end{equation*}
The second term is a Swish-shaped branch.  When $0\le\gamma\le1$,
\(\gamma\) interpolates between an identity branch and a Swish branch.  The
stored parameter is not constrained to that interval, so outside it the same
formula is an affine extrapolation rather than a convex interpolation.
$|\beta|$ sets how quickly the sigmoid gate changes around zero, while the
sign of \(\beta\) chooses whether it rises or falls.

For positive \(\beta\), the left limiting slope is $\gamma$ and the right is
one.  For negative \(\beta\), those two limits swap.  At \(\beta=0\), the
sigmoid is one half everywhere and both tails have the constant slope
$(1+\gamma)/2$.  In every case the tails remain linear.  This differs from
\code{tanh}, whose output approaches a constant and whose derivative
approaches zero on both tails.

The implementation initializes \(\gamma=0\) and \(\beta=0\).  Since
\(\sigma(0)=1/2\), every feature begins as \(H(x)=x/2\).  The initial map has
derivative \(1/2\) everywhere and therefore transmits a gradient even when
its input is zero.  Training then changes \(\gamma\) and \(\beta\)
independently for each feature.

\subsection{Multi-gate: learn several transitions in the bulk}

\(H\) contains one sigmoid transition fixed at the origin and ties the
positive-tail slope to one.  The multi-gate generalization replaces that
single transition with a sum of \(G\) learned sigmoids:
\begin{align}
 g_G(x)&=a_0+\sum_{g=1}^{G}
 w_g\,\sigma\!\left[\beta_g(x-\mu_g)\right],\nonumber\\
 A_G(x)&=g_G(x)x.
 \label{eq:multigate}
\end{align}
Here $G$ is the number of learned sigmoid transitions; it is unrelated to the
global output width $K$.  Each term has three roles.  \(w_g\) controls how much
the local slope schedule changes, $|\beta_g|$ controls how sharp the change is,
the sign of $\beta_g$ chooses whether the sigmoid rises or falls, and \(\mu_g\)
moves its center away from zero.  \(a_0\) supplies a baseline gate value.

The output remains asymptotically linear because every sigmoid is bounded.
A gate with $\beta_g>0$ contributes zero on the left and $w_g$ on the right;
a gate with $\beta_g<0$ contributes $w_g$ on the left and zero on the right;
a gate with $\beta_g=0$ contributes $w_g/2$ to both tails.  Therefore the
left and right limiting gate values are
\begin{align*}
 g_-&=a_0+\sum_{\beta_g<0}w_g
             +\frac12\sum_{\beta_g=0}w_g,\\
 g_+&=a_0+\sum_{\beta_g>0}w_g
             +\frac12\sum_{\beta_g=0}w_g,
\end{align*}
and $A_G(x)\sim g_-x$ on the left and $g_+x$ on the right.
The general form can therefore learn a piecewise-smooth slope schedule through
the bulk without inheriting \code{tanh}'s flat tails.

\(H\) is contained in Eq.~\eqref{eq:multigate}: choose \(G=1\),
\(a_0=\gamma\), \(w_1=1-\gamma\), \(\mu_1=0\), and
\(\beta_1=\beta\).  The implemented initialization instead uses a convenient
common start: \(a_0=0\), the first gate has \(w_1=1\) and \(\beta_1=0\), and
extra gates have zero weight.  The total gate is again \(1/2\), so the output
is \(x/2\).  Extra centers are spread across a finite interval and become
active only if training moves their initially zero weights.

\subsection{Bounded power tail: learn how rapidly the tails grow}

Multi-gate changes the slope schedule but retains linear tails.  The power
generalization changes a different property: the magnitude growth far from
zero.  First define the signed power transform
\begin{equation}
 \psi_p(x)=\operatorname{sign}(x)
 \frac{(1+|x|)^p-1}{p}.
 \label{eq:psi}
\end{equation}
The base \(1+|x|\) is at least one, so a fractional real exponent does not
take a power of a negative number.  Dividing by $p$ gives
$\psi'_p(0)=1$ for the mathematical function at every allowed exponent.
Consequently, changing $p$ reshapes the tail while preserving the analytic
local first-order behavior at zero.

That analytic statement and the derivative produced by the current PyTorch
expression are not identical at the single point $x=0$.  The implementation
multiplies by \code{torch.sign(x)} and evaluates \code{abs(x)}.  PyTorch uses
zero subgradients for these primitives at exactly zero, so autograd currently
reports a zero derivative there.  Forward values still satisfy
$\psi_1(x)=x$, but a zero-initialized correction can encounter the backward
exception before any feature moves away from zero.  The required liveness
contract therefore checks the derivative of the executed tensor expression,
not only Eq.~\eqref{eq:psi}; a repaired expression must retain the stated
forward law and transmit a finite nonzero first gradient at zero.

The exponent is not optimized as an unconstrained number.  The stored
parameter \(\rho\) is mapped through a sigmoid:
\begin{equation}
 p=p_{\min}+(p_{\max}-p_{\min})\sigma(\rho).
 \label{eq:pbox}
\end{equation}
With the default interval \([0.5,1.5]\), \(p\) ranges between
square-root-like sublinear growth and mildly superlinear growth.  At
\(\rho=0\), \(p=1\) and \(\psi_1(x)=x\).  The power activation is
\begin{equation}
 P(x)=\left[\gamma+(1-\gamma)\sigma(\beta x)\right]\psi_p(x).
 \label{eq:poweractivation}
\end{equation}
It therefore recovers \(H\) when \(p=1\).  This is a bounded generalization:
it allows the tail to adapt without allowing an arbitrary exponent to run to
a numerically explosive value.  The word \emph{bounded} describes the
exponent, not the floating-point result.  A sufficiently large finite
\code{float32} input can still overflow when \(p>1\).  The implementation
therefore needs both protections: constrain \(p\) at construction and reject
a nonfinite activation at the numerical boundary.  Neither protection makes
the other redundant.

\subsection{Gated power: combine bulk and tail freedom}

The two extensions act on orthogonal aspects of the curve.  Multi-gate
changes where and how the gate transitions in the central region.
The analytic function \(\psi_p\) changes the tail-growth law while keeping
unit slope in its mathematical limit at the origin.  The current
\code{sign}/\code{abs} tensor expression has the zero-backward exception
described above until its required liveness repair lands.
The combined activation is
\begin{equation}
  GP_G(x)=g_G(x)\psi_p(x).
  \label{eq:gatedpower}
\end{equation}
It is the most flexible implemented family and also has the most learned
shape vectors.  More flexibility is not automatically better: it increases
the number of parameters, enlarges the search space seen by the optimizer,
and makes an activation bake-off more important.

\begin{figure*}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.96\textwidth]
  {texnotes/figures/fig07_activations.pdf}
\caption{Representative activation curves.  Multiple gates can introduce
more than one transition in the bulk.  The bounded power changes the tail law
while the analytic signed transform preserves its local slope at zero.  The
current PyTorch expression's exact-zero backward derivative is instead zero,
as explained in the text; the plotted forward curve cannot display that
autograd exception.  The
plotted values illustrate mechanisms; learned parameters are feature-specific
and need not equal these examples.}
\label{fig:activations}
\end{figure*}

\subsection{Parameter count and qualitative comparison}

\begin{table*}[t]
\centering
\begin{tabular}{p{0.14\textwidth}p{0.14\textwidth}p{0.20\textwidth}
                p{0.20\textwidth}p{0.22\textwidth}}
\toprule
Family & Learned scalars per feature & Bulk behavior & Tail behavior &
Primary comparison question \\
\midrule
\(H\) & \(2\) & One signed transition centered at zero & Linear; slopes are
$(\gamma,1)$ for $\beta>0$, swapped for $\beta<0$ & Is the compact non-saturating default
sufficient? \\
Multi-gate & \(3G+1\) & \(G\) movable transitions & Linear, both limiting
slopes learned & Is one central bend too restrictive? \\
Power & \(3\) & The same single gate as \(H\) & Signed
\(|x|^p\)-like growth with bounded \(p\) & Does tail curvature, rather than
bulk gating, limit the fit? \\
Gated power & \(3G+2\) & \(G\) movable transitions & Bounded learnable power
growth & Do both restrictions matter together? \\
ReLU & \(0\) & One hard kink; negative side exactly zero & Zero on the left,
linear on the right & How does a parameter-free rectifier compare? \\
\(\tanh\) & \(0\) & Smooth S-curve & Saturates to constants & How does a
classical smooth but saturating baseline compare? \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\caption{The implemented activation families.  Per feature means that a layer
of width \(W\) owns the listed count multiplied by \(W\).}
\label{tab:activation-compare}
\end{table*}

The count matters because a network creates a fresh activation module at
every activation site.  For width \(W=128\), \(G=3\), and \(L\) sites,
multi-gate adds
\begin{equation*}
  (3G+1)WL=1280L
\end{equation*}
learned scalars.  Those values are small compared with a
\(128\times128\) dense matrix at each site, but they are not free, and their
gradients enlarge the optimization problem.

\subsection{PyTorch shape mechanics in the multi-gate code}

For an input of shape \shape{(B,W)}, the multi-gate parameter tensors have
shape \shape{(G,W)}.  The code calls \code{x.unsqueeze(-2)}, which inserts a
size-one axis immediately before the feature axis:
\begin{equation*}
  \shape{(B,W)}\longrightarrow\shape{(B,1,W)}.
\end{equation*}
Broadcasting against \shape{(G,W)} then produces
\shape{(B,G,W)} without explicitly copying the input \(G\) times.  The
sigmoid is evaluated for every sample, gate, and feature.  Summing over axis
\code{-2}, the gate axis, returns \shape{(B,W)}.  Finally the gate is
multiplied elementwise by the original input.

This implementation is eager tensor algebra: every expression executes when
\code{forward} is called.  It is not a Python generator and does not use
\code{yield}.  The parameter tensors live on the same device and use the same
dtype as the module because they are registered \code{nn.Parameter} objects.

\subsection{How the family is selected and persisted}

\code{make\_activation(name,n\_gates)} returns a factory with the interface
\code{act(dim)} returning a new module.  For \code{relu} and \code{tanh}, the
factory's \code{dim} argument is unused because those classes have no
per-feature parameters.  Keeping the same callable interface lets
residual-block construction remain uniform.

The resolved artifact stores the family name and gate count.  Rebuilding
calls the same factory before loading the state dictionary.  This order is
required because the state dictionary contains tensor values but not the
Python class that gives them meaning.  A warm start inherits the source
artifact's activation family; silently rebuilding identical tensor shapes
under a different formula would create a different function while making
weight loading appear successful.

\subsection{A fair activation comparison}

An activation bake-off holds the data subset, model architecture, optimizer
rules, schedule, random-seed policy, and evaluation statistic fixed while
changing only the activation family.  One final score is insufficient.  The
driver trains each family at several values of \(N_{\rm train}\) and plots
the validation failure fraction against training-set size.  A family whose
curve is lower across the range learns more accurately at the same simulation
budget; curves that cross reveal a data-regime dependence.

The comparison also checks liveness.  A structured correction head that
remains equal to its trunk may still produce a respectable aggregate score.
The gate must therefore verify that at least one head weight receives a
nonzero finite gradient and changes after an optimizer step.  This is
especially important for ReLU: its derivative at exactly zero is zero, so a
ReLU placed after an exactly zero-initialized final head layer can make the
head permanently inactive even though the trunk continues to learn.

\section{The loss: where network error becomes a scientific error}
\label{chap:loss}

The model emits network coordinates.  The loss decides whether their error is
acceptable in the physical likelihood.  It is therefore not an interchangeable
training convenience.

\subsection{Plain covariance-weighted loss}

For a prediction $\widehat T$ and encoded truth $T$, both with batch-first
network shape, the geometry can return both to physical kept coordinates.
The residual matrix is
\begin{equation}
  R = \widehat V_{\rm keep}-V_{\rm keep}
  \in\mathbb R^{B\times K}.
\end{equation}
The slice $r_i=R[i:i+1,:]$ preserves a length-one batch axis and has shape
$(1,K)$.  Integer indexing $R[i,:]$ would instead remove that axis and return
shape $(K,)$.  With
